(Uruguay)

B
Reply of Montevideo Police Department to Questionnaire submitted
June
20,
1924
(Translated by Office of the Ministry of Foreign Affairs)
1.
There exists
a traffic in women, nearly all coming from
Europe,
and engaged in by well-known international oaftens (pimps-
souteneurs).
But
such traffic does not have Uruguay for its final
destination,
as most of the business is directed to Buenos Aires^
Those
women arriving in this country as
a consequence of the trade
in the Argentine capital,
come in under conditions radically differ¬
ent from those affecting women who are deceived and carried frcm
their homes by "maquereaux".
Although the
women are controlled
by such persons, in Uruguay they (women)
are already prostitutes
with a perfect knowledge of the kind of business they are required
to engage in.
This circumstances make more difficult the action
of the Bolide,
as it finds
persons with knowledge of the business
and also those
who
are terrified under compulsion of their exploit¬
ers.
This trade is
clandestine.
2.
The traffic does not exist in this
manner.
Sometimes
it happens that prostitutes leave the country, alone or with men
that live
upon them.
They go by preference to Argentine
or Brazil.
But
a real traffic
does not exist,
and the voyage is made in order
to
look for better conditions (outlook), but always in the
same
kind of
a life
as here.
3.
As traffic fr cm abroad to Uruguay does not exist,
ex¬
cept
as before stated,
-
severe steps are taken by the Government
to prevent it by adher^ing to measures taken by other Governments,
and by enacting special legislation in that respect.
By the Law of June
18,
1920,
the Legislative Power gave au¬
thorization to
the Presidency of the Republic to adhere to the In¬
ternational Convention concerning the repression of 7/hite Slave
Traffio, held in Paris
on May 4,
191X).
(Note:
On May 21,
1924,
the Presidency of the Republic submitted to the Parliament a Bill
demanding its authorization to adhere to the International Conven¬
tion concerning the suppression of the Traffic in Womenand Child¬
ren,
concluded at Geneva on Sept. 30,
1921—Note of Foreign Office).
On Oct. 20,
1916 there was approved the
"Brum Act" concern¬
ing the repression of proxenetism and other similar offenses, modi¬
fying previous provisions, indicating who are guilty of these of¬
fenses, providing puniaimients
( 2 to 4 years,
12 to 15 months of
imprisonment and 3 to
6 months of the
same or an equivalent fine^ i
etc., etc.
```

C
Montevideo,
July 1st,
1924»
Mr.
Carlos Ferrando,
Secretary General, Police Department,
Montevideo, Uruguay,
Dear Sir:
I have received your memorandum of June 24th, 1924, giving
answers to
seven of the list of ten preliminary questions handed you
"by me
on June
20th.
I wish to
thank you very much, not only
on my
own "behalf,
"but also
on "behalf of
the Special Commission of Experts appointed by
the League of Dations to investigate the Traffic in Women and Child¬
ren in collaboration with
Governments#
¥our intelligent answers will
prove of great assistance to this International Commission in grappling
with this
grave International problem.
I
now beg leave to avail myself of the permission already
granted by you at our first interview,
of submitting a further list
of questions which arise naturally from
a study of your answers to
the first list, and also from my several very helpful conferences
with the officials
of the Immigration Service,
and with the President
and other officials of the national Council of Hygiene.
In asking
these questions I will base them on the first list of questions,
numbering each new question with the
same number as the question in
the first list
on which it is based,
1(a).
I understand you to state in
answer to my first ques¬
tion that, although it is true that alien women are brought by "well
known international caftens" frcm Europe to Uruguay for purposes of
prostitution, neither the caftens nor their victims remain long in
Uruguay, but proceed to Buenos Aires, which is the center of this
international traffic.
If I
an correct in my understanding of your
answer,
may I further inquire what measures the Uruguayan Government
takes
or contemplates taking, either alone or in cooperation with the
Argentine Government to prevent the use by such traffickers of Uruguay
and especially of Montevideo as
a way station either by sxich traffick¬
ers
or by their women victims?
1(b).
In this connection, is it true that
such traffickers
and their victims
can
and
do enter Montevideo from Bio de
Janeiro by
railroad through Bivera without passports or other papers, and that
many of these individuals, after a short stay in Montevideo proceed,
still without documents, by train to Salto and other border stations,
and cross
the river into Argentine at those points by private launch,
easily evading the Uruguayan and Argentine authorities?
```
